</p><p>Near to the end of the battle for <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a>, the Shah fled rather than surrender. Genghis Khan subsequently ordered two of his generals, <a href="/wiki/Subutai" title="Subutai">Subutai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jebe" title="Jebe">Jebe</a>, to destroy the remnants of the Khwarazmian Empire, giving them 20,000 men and two years to do this. The Shah died under mysterious circumstances on a small island in the Caspian Sea that he had retreated to with his remaining loyal forces.
</p><p>Meanwhile, the wealthy trading city of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Konya-Urgench" title="Konya-Urgench">Urgench</a> was still in the hands of Khwarazmian forces. The assault on Urgench proved to be the most difficult battle of the Mongol invasion and the city fell only after the defenders put up a stout defense, fighting block for block. Mongolian casualties were higher than normal, due to the unaccustomed difficulty of adapting Mongolian tactics to city fighting.
As usual, the artisans were sent back to Mongolia, young women and children were given to the Mongol soldiers as slaves, and the rest of the population was massacred. The Persian scholar <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ata_al-Mulk_Juvayni" title="Ata al-Mulk Juvayni">Juvayni</a> states that 50,000 Mongol soldiers were given the task of executing twenty-four Urgench citizens each, which would mean that 1.2 million people were killed. These numbers are considered logistically implausible by modern scholars, but the sacking of Urgench was no doubt a bloody affair.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_August_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(August_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_18-5'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_August_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(August_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-18'>[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span id="Georgia.2C_Crimea.2C_Kievan_Rus_and_Volga_Bulgaria"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Georgia,_Crimea,_Kievan_Rus_and_Volga_Bulgaria">Georgia, Crimea, Kievan Rus and Volga Bulgaria</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasions_of_Georgia_and_Armenia" title="Mongol invasions of Georgia and Armenia">Mongol invasions of Georgia and Armenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Volga_Bulgaria" title="Mongol invasion of Volga Bulgaria">Mongol invasion of Volga Bulgaria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan,_struck_at_the_Ghazna_(Ghazni)_mint.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="384" data-file-width="800" decoding="async" height="106" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg/220px-Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg/330px-Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f3/Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg/440px-Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan%2C_struck_at_the_Ghazna_%28Ghazni%29_mint.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Gold_coin_of_Genghis_Khan,_struck_at_the_Ghazna_(Ghazni)_mint.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Gold <i><a href="/wiki/Gold_dinar" title="Gold dinar">dinar</a></i> of Genghis Khan, struck at the <a href="/wiki/Ghazni" title="Ghazni">Ghazna</a> (Ghazni) mint, dated 1221/2</div></div></div>
<p>After the defeat of the Khwarazmian Empire in 1220, Genghis Khan gathered his forces in <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Persia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> to return to the Mongolian steppes. Under the suggestion of <a href="/wiki/Subutai" title="Subutai">Subutai</a>, the Mongol army was split into two forces. Genghis Khan led the main army on a raid through <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a> and northern India towards Mongolia, while another 20,000 (two <a href="/wiki/Tumen_(unit)" title="Tumen (unit)">tumen</a>) contingent marched through the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a> and into Russia under generals <a href="/wiki/Jebe" title="Jebe">Jebe</a> and Subutai. They pushed deep into <a href="/wiki/Armenia" title="Armenia">Armenia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a>. The Mongols defeated the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Georgia" title="Kingdom of Georgia">kingdom of Georgia</a>, sacked the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Genoa" title="Republic of Genoa">Genoese</a> trade-fortress of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Caffa" title="Caffa">Caffa</a> in <a href="/wiki/Crimea" title="Crimea">Crimea</a> and overwintered near the <a href="/wiki/Black_Sea" title="Black Sea">Black Sea</a>. Heading home, Subutai's forces attacked the allied forces of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cuman" title="Cuman">Cuman</a>–<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kipchak_people" title="Kipchak people">Kipchaks</a> and the poorly coordinated 80,000 <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a> troops led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mstislav_the_Bold" title="Mstislav the Bold">Mstislav the Bold</a> of <a href="/wiki/Halych" title="Halych">Halych</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mstislav_III_of_Kiev" title="Mstislav III of Kiev">Mstislav III of Kiev</a> who went out to stop the Mongols' actions in the area. <a href="/wiki/Subutai" title="Subutai">Subutai</a> sent emissaries to the <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavic</a> princes calling for a separate peace, but the emissaries were executed. At the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Kalka_River" title="Battle of Kalka River">Battle of Kalka River</a> in 1223, Subutai's forces defeated the larger Kievan force. They may have been defeated by the neighbouring <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Volga_Bulgars" title="Volga Bulgars">Volga Bulgars</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Samara_Bend" title="Battle of Samara Bend">Battle of Samara Bend</a>. There is no historical record except a short account by the Arab historian <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-Athir" title="Ibn al-Athir">Ibn al-Athir</a>, writing in Mosul some 1,800 kilometres (1,100 miles) away from the event.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chambers31_70-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chambers31-70">[67]</a></sup> Various historical secondary sources – Morgan, Chambers, Grousset – state that the Mongols actually defeated the Bulgars, Chambers even going so far as to say that the Bulgars had made up stories to tell the (recently crushed) Russians that they had beaten the Mongols and driven them from their territory.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chambers31_70-1"><a href="#cite_note-Chambers31-70">[67]</a></sup> The Russian princes then sued for peace. Subutai agreed but was in no mood to pardon the princes. Not only had the Rus put up strong resistance, but also Jebe – with whom Subutai had campaigned for years – had been killed just prior to the Battle of Kalka River.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[68]</a></sup> As was customary in Mongol society for nobility, the Russian princes were given a bloodless death. Subutai had a large wooden platform constructed on which he ate his meals along with his other generals. Six Russian princes, including <a href="/wiki/Mstislav_III_of_Kiev" title="Mstislav III of Kiev">Mstislav III of Kiev</a>, were put under this platform and crushed to death.
</p><p>The Mongols learned from captives of the abundant green pastures beyond the Bulgar territory, allowing for the planning for <a href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_Europe" title="Mongol invasion of Europe">conquest of Hungary and Europe</a>. Genghis Khan recalled Subutai back to Mongolia soon afterwards. The famous cavalry expedition led by Subutai and Jebe, in which they encircled the entire Caspian Sea defeating all armies in their path, remains unparalleled to this day, and word of the Mongol triumphs began to trickle to other nations, particularly in Europe. These two campaigns are generally regarded as reconnaissance campaigns that tried to get the feel of the political and cultural elements of the regions. In 1225 both divisions returned to Mongolia. These invasions added <a href="/wiki/Transoxiana" title="Transoxiana">Transoxiana</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Persia</a> to an already formidable empire while destroying any resistance along the way. Later under Genghis Khan's grandson <a href="/wiki/Batu_Khan" title="Batu Khan">Batu</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Golden_Horde" title="Golden Horde">Golden Horde</a>, the Mongols returned to conquer Volga Bulgaria and Kievan Rus' in 1237, concluding the campaign in 1240.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Western_Xia_and_Jin_Dynasty">Western Xia and Jin Dynasty</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_invasion_of_China" title="Mongol invasion of China">Mongol invasion of China</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_China_1142.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="560" decoding="async" height="216" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Map_of_China_1142.jpg/220px-Map_of_China_1142.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Map_of_China_1142.jpg/330px-Map_of_China_1142.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Map_of_China_1142.jpg/440px-Map_of_China_1142.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Map_of_China_1142.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Western_Xia" title="Western Xia">Western Xia</a> dynasty, <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin/Jurchen dynasty</a>, <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Dali" title="Kingdom of Dali">Kingdom of Dali</a> in 1142</div></div></div>
<p>The vassal emperor of the Tanguts (Western Xia) had earlier refused to take part in the Mongol war against the Khwarezmid Empire. Western Xia and the defeated Jin dynasty formed a coalition to resist the Mongols, counting on the campaign against the Khwarazmians to preclude the Mongols from responding effectively.
</p><p>In 1226, immediately after returning from the west, Genghis Khan began a retaliatory attack on the Tanguts. His armies quickly took Heisui, <a href="/wiki/Zhangye" title="Zhangye">Ganzhou</a>, and Suzhou (not the Suzhou in Jiangsu province), and in the autumn he took <a href="/wiki/Western_Liang_(Sixteen_Kingdoms)" title="Western Liang (Sixteen Kingdoms)">Xiliang</a>-fu. One of the Tangut generals challenged the Mongols to a battle near <a href="/wiki/Helan_Mountains" title="Helan Mountains">Helan Mountains</a> but was defeated. In November, Genghis laid <a href="/wiki/Siege" title="Siege">siege</a> to the Tangut city <a href="/wiki/Lingwu" title="Lingwu">Lingzhou</a> and crossed the <a href="/wiki/Yellow_River" title="Yellow River">Yellow River</a>, defeating the Tangut relief army. According to legend, it was here that Genghis Khan reportedly saw a line of five stars arranged in the sky and interpreted it as an omen of his victory.
</p><p>In 1227, Genghis Khan's army attacked and destroyed the Tangut capital of Ning Hia and continued to advance, seizing <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Lintiao&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Lintiao (page does not exist)">Lintiao</a>-fu, <a href="/wiki/Xining" title="Xining">Xining</a> province, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Xindu" title="Xindu">Xindu</a>-fu, and <a href="/wiki/Deshun" title="Deshun">Deshun</a> province in quick succession in the spring. At Deshun, the Tangut general Ma Jianlong put up a fierce resistance for several days and personally led charges against the invaders outside the city gate. Ma Jianlong later died from wounds received from arrows in battle. Genghis Khan, after conquering Deshun, went to <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Liupanshan&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Liupanshan (page does not exist)">Liupanshan</a> (<a href="/wiki/Qingshui_County" title="Qingshui County">Qingshui County</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gansu" title="Gansu">Gansu</a> Province) to escape the severe summer. The new Tangut emperor quickly surrendered to the Mongols, and the rest of the Tanguts officially surrendered soon after. Not happy with their betrayal and resistance, Genghis Khan ordered the entire imperial family to be executed, effectively ending the Tangut royal lineage.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Death_and_succession">Death and succession</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Genghis_khan_empire_at_his_death.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="752" decoding="async" height="173" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Genghis_khan_empire_at_his_death.png/220px-Genghis_khan_empire_at_his_death.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Genghis_khan_empire_at_his_death.png/330px-Genghis_khan_empire_at_his_death.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Genghis_khan_empire_at_his_death.png/440px-Genghis_khan_empire_at_his_death.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Genghis_khan_empire_at_his_death.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mongol Empire in 1227 at Genghis Khan's death</div></div></div>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tomb_of_Genghis_Khan" title="Tomb of Genghis Khan">Tomb of Genghis Khan</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Mongol_rulers" title="List of Mongol rulers">List of Mongol rulers</a></div>
<p>Genghis Khan died within eight days of setting off for his final campaign against the <a href="/wiki/Western_Xia" title="Western Xia">Western Xia</a> on 18 August 1227, according to the official <i>History of Yuan</i> commissioned during China's Ming dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cho21_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cho21-72">[69]</a></sup> The date of his death is therefore said to have fallen on 25 August 1227, during the fall of <a href="/wiki/Yinchuan" title="Yinchuan">Yinchuan</a>. The exact cause of his death remains a mystery, and is variously attributed to illness, being killed in action or from wounds sustained in hunting or battle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-china_at_war_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-china_at_war-73">[70]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMan2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidOXTv9a0HZakCpgPA239_239–240]_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMan2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidOXTv9a0HZakCpgPA239_239–240]-75">[72]</a></sup> According to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_History_of_the_Mongols" title="The Secret History of the Mongols">The Secret History of the Mongols</a></i>, Genghis Khan fell from his horse while hunting and died because of the injury. The <i><a href="/wiki/Galician%E2%80%93Volhynian_Chronicle" title="Galician–Volhynian Chronicle">Galician–Volhynian Chronicle</a></i> alleges he was killed by the Western Xia in battle, while <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> wrote that he died after the infection of an arrow wound he received during his final campaign.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[73]</a></sup> Later Mongol chronicles connect Genghis's death with a Western Xia princess taken as war booty. One chronicle from the early 17th century even relates the legend that the princess hid a small dagger and stabbed or castrated him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[74]</a></sup> All of these legends were invented well after Genghis Khan's death, however.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cho21_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cho21-72">[69]</a></sup> In contrast, a 2021 study found that the great leader likely died from <a href="/wiki/Bubonic_plague" title="Bubonic plague">bubonic plague</a>, after investigating reports of the clinical signs exhibited by both the Khan and his army, which in turn matched the symptoms associated with the strain of plague present in Western Xia at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[75]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:CoronationOfOgodei1229.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2006" data-file-width="1420" decoding="async" height="311" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/CoronationOfOgodei1229.jpg/220px-CoronationOfOgodei1229.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/CoronationOfOgodei1229.jpg/330px-CoronationOfOgodei1229.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/CoronationOfOgodei1229.jpg/440px-CoronationOfOgodei1229.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:CoronationOfOgodei1229.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Genghis Khan (center) at the coronation of his son Ögedei, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashid-al-Din_Hamadani" title="Rashid-al-Din Hamadani">Rashid al-Din</a></i>, early 14th century</div></div></div>
<p>Years before his death, Genghis Khan asked to be buried without markings, according to the customs of his tribe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMan2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidOXTv9a0HZakCpgPA254_254–255]_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMan2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidOXTv9a0HZakCpgPA254_254–255]-79">[76]</a></sup> After he died, his body was returned to <a href="/wiki/Mongolia" title="Mongolia">Mongolia</a> and presumably to his birthplace in <a href="/wiki/Khentii_Province" title="Khentii Province">Khentii Aimag</a>, where many assume he is buried somewhere close to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Onon_River" title="Onon River">Onon River</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Burkhan_Khaldun" title="Burkhan Khaldun">Burkhan Khaldun</a> mountain (part of the Khentii mountain range). According to legend, the funeral escort killed anyone and anything across their path to conceal where he was finally buried. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan_Mausoleum" title="Genghis Khan Mausoleum">Genghis Khan Mausoleum</a>, constructed many years after his death, is his memorial, but not his burial site.
</p><p>Before Genghis Khan died, he assigned <a href="/wiki/%C3%96gedei_Khan" title="Ögedei Khan">Ögedei Khan</a> as his successor. Genghis Khan left behind an army of more than 129,000 men; 28,000 were given to his various brothers and his sons. Tolui, his youngest son, inherited more than 100,000 men. This force contained the bulk of the elite Mongolian <a href="/wiki/Cavalry" title="Cavalry">cavalry</a>. By tradition, the youngest son inherits his father's property. <a href="/wiki/Jochi" title="Jochi">Jochi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khan" title="Chagatai Khan">Chagatai</a>, <a href="/wiki/%C3%96gedei_Khan" title="Ögedei Khan">Ögedei Khan</a>, and Kulan's son Gelejian received armies of 4,000 men each. His mother and the descendants of his three brothers received 3,000 men each. The title of Great Khan passed to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%C3%96gedei" title="Ögedei">Ögedei</a>, the third son of Genghis Khan, making him the second Great Khan (<a href="/wiki/Khagan" title="Khagan">Khagan</a>) of the Mongol Empire. Genghis Khan's eldest son, Jochi, died in 1226, during his father's lifetime. Chagatai, Genghis Khan's second son was meanwhile passed over, according to <i><a href="/wiki/The_Secret_History_of_the_Mongols" title="The Secret History of the Mongols">The Secret History of the Mongols</a></i>, over a row just before the invasion of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khwarezmid_Empire" title="Khwarezmid Empire">Khwarezmid Empire</a> in which Chagatai declared before his father and brothers that he would never accept Jochi as Genghis Khan's successor due to questions about his elder brother's parentage. In response to this tension and possibly for other reasons, Ögedei was appointed as successor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTERatchnevsky1991126_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTERatchnevsky1991126-80">[77]</a></sup>
</p><p>Later, his grandsons <a href="/wiki/Division_of_the_Mongol_Empire" title="Division of the Mongol Empire">split his empire</a> into <a href="/wiki/Khanate" title="Khanate">khanates</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[78]</a></sup> His <a href="/wiki/Descent_from_Genghis_Khan" title="Descent from Genghis Khan">descendants</a> extended the Mongol Empire across most of Eurasia by conquering or creating vassal states in all of modern-day China, <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korea</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Caucasus" title="Caucasus">Caucasus</a>, Central Asia, and substantial portions of Eastern Europe and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Southwest_Asia" title="Southwest Asia">Southwest Asia</a>. Many of these invasions repeated the earlier large-scale slaughters of local populations.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Organizational_philosophy">Organizational philosophy</span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Organization_of_the_Mongol_Empire_under_Genghis_Khan" title="Organization of the Mongol Empire under Genghis Khan">Organization of the Mongol Empire under Genghis Khan</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Politics_and_economics">Politics and economics</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mongol_Empire_map.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="591" data-file-width="752" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Mongol_Empire_map.gif/280px-Mongol_Empire_map.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Mongol_Empire_map.gif/420px-Mongol_Empire_map.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Mongol_Empire_map.gif/560px-Mongol_Empire_map.gif 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mongol_Empire_map.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Expansion of the <a href="/wiki/Mongol_Empire" title="Mongol Empire">Mongol Empire</a> 1206–1294</div></div></div>
<p>The Mongol Empire was governed by a civilian and military <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Code_(law)" title="Code (law)">code</a>, called the <a href="/wiki/Yassa" title="Yassa">Yassa</a>, created by Genghis Khan. The Mongol Empire did not emphasize the importance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ethnicity" title="Ethnicity">ethnicity</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Race_(classification_of_human_beings)" title="Race (classification of human beings)">race</a> in the administrative realm, instead adopting an approach grounded in <a href="/wiki/Meritocracy" title="Meritocracy">meritocracy</a>.<sup class="reference" id='cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2004[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_August_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(August_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;_82-0'><a href='#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2004[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_August_2021]]&lt;sup_class="noprint_Inline-Template_"_style="white-space:nowrap;"&gt;&amp;#91;&lt;i&gt;[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|&lt;span_title="This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.&amp;#32;(August_2021)"&gt;page&amp;nbsp;needed&lt;/span&gt;]]&lt;/i&gt;&amp;#93;&lt;/sup&gt;-82'>[79]</a></sup> The Mongol Empire was one of the most ethnically and culturally diverse empires in history, as befitted its size. Many of the empire's nomadic inhabitants considered themselves <i>Mongols</i> in military and civilian life, including the <a href="/wiki/Mongols" title="Mongols">Mongol</a> people, <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> peoples, and others. There were <a href="/wiki/Khan_(title)" title="Khan (title)">Khans</a> of various non-Mongolian ethnicities such as <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Khan_(Ilkhan)" title="Muhammad Khan (Ilkhan)">Muhammad Khan</a>.
</p><p>There were tax exemptions for religious figures and, to some extent, teachers and doctors. The Mongol Empire practiced <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" title="Religious tolerance">religious tolerance</a> because Mongol tradition had long held that religion was a personal concept, and not subject to law or interference.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[80]</a></sup> Genghis Khan was a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tengrist" title="Tengrist">Tengrist</a>, but was <a href="/wiki/Religious_tolerance" title="Religious tolerance">religiously tolerant</a> and interested in learning philosophical and moral lessons from other religions. He consulted <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhist</a> monks (including the Zen monk Haiyun), <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Muslims</a>, <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">Christian</a> missionaries, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daoism" title="Daoism">Daoist</a> monk <a href="/wiki/Qiu_Chuji" title="Qiu Chuji">Qiu Chuji</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[81]</a></sup> Sometime before the rise of Genghis Khan, Ong Khan, his mentor and eventual rival, had converted to <a href="/wiki/Nestorianism" title="Nestorianism">Nestorian Christianity</a>. Various Mongol tribes were Shamanist, Buddhist or Christian. Religious tolerance was thus a well established concept on the Asian steppe.
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Religion_in_the_Mongol_Empire" title="Religion in the Mongol Empire">Religion in the Mongol Empire</a></div>
<p>Modern Mongolian historians say that towards the end of his life, Genghis Khan attempted to create a <a href="/wiki/Civilian" title="Civilian">civil state</a> under the Great Yassa that would have established the legal equality of all individuals, including <a href="/wiki/Women%27s_rights" title="Women's rights">women</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[82]</a></sup> However, there is no evidence of this, or of the lifting of discriminatory policies towards <a href="/wiki/Sedentism" title="Sedentism">sedentary</a> peoples such as the Chinese. Women played a relatively important role in the Mongol Empire and in the family, for example <a href="/wiki/T%C3%B6regene_Khatun" title="Töregene Khatun">Töregene Khatun</a> was briefly in charge of the Mongol Empire while the next male leader <a href="/wiki/Khagan" title="Khagan">Khagan</a> was being chosen. Modern scholars refer to the alleged policy of encouraging trade and communication as the <a href="/wiki/Pax_Mongolica" title="Pax Mongolica">Pax Mongolica</a> (Mongol Peace).
</p><p>Genghis Khan realised that he needed people who could govern cities and states conquered by him. He also realised that such administrators could not be found among his Mongol people because they were nomads and thus had no experience governing cities. For this purpose Genghis Khan invited a <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitan</a> prince, <a href="/wiki/Yel%C3%BC_Chucai" title="Yelü Chucai">Chu'Tsai</a>, who worked for the Jin and had been captured by the Mongol army after the Jin dynasty was defeated. Jin had risen to power by displacing the Khitan people. Genghis told Chu'Tsai, who was a lineal descendant of Khitan rulers, that he had avenged Chu'Tsai's forefathers. Chu'Tsai responded that his father served the Jin dynasty honestly and so did he; also he did not consider his own father his enemy, so the question of revenge did not apply. This reply impressed Genghis Khan. Chu'Tsai administered parts of the Mongol Empire and became a confidant of the successive Mongol Khans.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (August 2021)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mural_of_siege_warfare,_Genghis_Khan_Exhibit,_Tech_Museum_San_Jose,_2010.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1936" data-file-width="2592" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mural_of_siege_warfare%2C_Genghis_Khan_Exhibit%2C_Tech_Museum_San_Jose%2C_2010.jpg/220px-Mural_of_siege_warfare%2C_Genghis_Khan_Exhibit%2C_Tech_Museum_San_Jose%2C_2010.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mural_of_siege_warfare%2C_Genghis_Khan_Exhibit%2C_Tech_Museum_San_Jose%2C_2010.jpg/330px-Mural_of_siege_warfare%2C_Genghis_Khan_Exhibit%2C_Tech_Museum_San_Jose%2C_2010.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Mural_of_siege_warfare%2C_Genghis_Khan_Exhibit%2C_Tech_Museum_San_Jose%2C_2010.jpg/440px-Mural_of_siege_warfare%2C_Genghis_Khan_Exhibit%2C_Tech_Museum_San_Jose%2C_2010.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mural_of_siege_warfare,_Genghis_Khan_Exhibit,_Tech_Museum_San_Jose,_2010.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Mural of siege warfare, Genghis Khan Exhibit in San Jose, California, US</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mock_Mongolian_battle_at_Khaan_Quest_07.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3008" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mock_Mongolian_battle_at_Khaan_Quest_07.jpg/220px-Mock_Mongolian_battle_at_Khaan_Quest_07.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mock_Mongolian_battle_at_Khaan_Quest_07.jpg/330px-Mock_Mongolian_battle_at_Khaan_Quest_07.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Mock_Mongolian_battle_at_Khaan_Quest_07.jpg/440px-Mock_Mongolian_battle_at_Khaan_Quest_07.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mock_Mongolian_battle_at_Khaan_Quest_07.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Reenactment of Mongol battle</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Military">Military</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Mongol_military_tactics_and_organization" title="Mongol military tactics and organization">Mongol military tactics and organization</a></div>
<p>Genghis Khan put absolute trust in his generals, such as <a href="/wiki/Muqali" title="Muqali">Muqali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jebe" title="Jebe">Jebe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Subutai" title="Subutai">Subutai</a>, and regarded them as close advisors, often extending them the same privileges and trust normally reserved for close family members. He allowed them to make decisions on their own when they embarked on campaigns far from the Mongol Empire capital <a href="/wiki/Karakorum" title="Karakorum">Karakorum</a>. Muqali, a trusted lieutenant, was given command of the Mongol forces against the Jin dynasty while Genghis Khan was fighting in Central Asia, and <a href="/wiki/Subutai" title="Subutai">Subutai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jebe" title="Jebe">Jebe</a> were allowed to pursue the Great Raid into the Caucasus and <a href="/wiki/Kievan_Rus%27" title="Kievan Rus'">Kievan Rus'</a>, an idea they had presented to the Khagan on their own initiative. While granting his generals a great deal of autonomy in making command decisions, Genghis Khan also expected unwavering loyalty from them.
</p><p>The Mongol military was also successful in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Siege_warfare" title="Siege warfare">siege warfare</a>, cutting off resources for cities and towns by diverting certain rivers, taking enemy prisoners and driving them in front of the army,  and adopting new ideas, techniques and tools from the people they conquered, particularly in employing Muslim and Chinese siege engines and engineers to aid the Mongol cavalry in capturing cities. Another standard <a href="/wiki/Military_tactics" title="Military tactics">tactic</a> of the Mongol military was the commonly practiced <a href="/wiki/Withdrawal_(military)" title="Withdrawal (military)">feigned retreat</a> to break enemy formations and to lure small enemy groups away from the larger group and defended position for <a href="/wiki/Ambush" title="Ambush">ambush</a> and <a href="/wiki/Counterattack" title="Counterattack">counterattack</a>.
</p><p>Another important aspect of the military organization of Genghis Khan was the <a href="/wiki/Transport" title="Transport">communications</a> and <a href="/wiki/Materiel" title="Materiel">supply</a> route or <i><a href="/wiki/Yam_(route)" title="Yam (route)">Yam</a></i>, adapted from previous Chinese models. Genghis Khan dedicated special attention to this in order to speed up the gathering of <a href="/wiki/Military_intelligence" title="Military intelligence">military intelligence</a> and official communications. To this end, Yam waystations were established all over the empire.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeatherford200458_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeatherford200458-86">[83]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Impressions">Impressions</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Positive">Positive</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GhingisHan-rev.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="304" data-file-width="304" decoding="async" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/GhingisHan-rev.jpg/170px-GhingisHan-rev.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/be/GhingisHan-rev.jpg/255px-GhingisHan-rev.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/be/GhingisHan-rev.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GhingisHan-rev.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Genghis Khan on the reverse of a <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstan" title="Kazakhstan">Kazakh</a> 100 <a href="/wiki/Kazakhstani_tenge" title="Kazakhstani tenge"><i>tenge</i></a> collectible coin.</div></div></div>
<p>Genghis Khan is credited with bringing the <a href="/wiki/Silk_Road" title="Silk Road">Silk Road</a> under one cohesive political environment. This allowed increased communication and trade between the West, Middle East and Asia, thus expanding the horizons of all three cultural areas. Some historians have noted that Genghis Khan instituted certain levels of <a href="/wiki/Meritocracy" title="Meritocracy">meritocracy</a> in his rule, was tolerant of religions and explained his policies clearly to all his soldiers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[84]</a></sup> Genghis Khan had a notably positive reputation among some western European authors in the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, who knew little concrete information about his empire in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidA8Y9B5uHQcACpgPA239_239]_88-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidA8Y9B5uHQcACpgPA239_239]-88">[85]</a></sup> The Italian explorer <a href="/wiki/Marco_Polo" title="Marco Polo">Marco Polo</a> said that Genghis Khan "was a man of great worth, and of great ability, and valor",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[87]</a></sup> while philosopher and inventor <a href="/wiki/Roger_Bacon" title="Roger Bacon">Roger Bacon</a> applauded the scientific and philosophical vigor of Genghis Khan's empire,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidA8Y9B5uHQcACpgPR24_xxiv]_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidA8Y9B5uHQcACpgPR24_xxiv]-91">[88]</a></sup> and the famed writer <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a> wrote concerning <span title="Middle English (1100-1500)-language text"><i lang="enm">Cambinskan</i></span>:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chaucer_92-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chaucer-92">[89]</a></sup>
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><div class="poem">
<p>The noble king was called Genghis Khan,<br/>
Who in his time was of so great renown,<br/>
That there was nowhere in no region,<br/>
So excellent a lord in all things
</p>
</div></blockquote>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Chinggis_Khan_hillside_portrait.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2304" data-file-width="3072" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Chinggis_Khan_hillside_portrait.JPG/220px-Chinggis_Khan_hillside_portrait.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Chinggis_Khan_hillside_portrait.JPG/330px-Chinggis_Khan_hillside_portrait.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Chinggis_Khan_hillside_portrait.JPG/440px-Chinggis_Khan_hillside_portrait.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Chinggis_Khan_hillside_portrait.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Portrait on a hillside in Ulaanbaatar, 2006</div></div></div>
<p>In Mongolia, Genghis Khan has meanwhile been revered for centuries by Mongols and many <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic peoples</a> because of his association with tribal statehood, political and military organization, and victories in war. As the principal unifying figure in Mongolian history, he remains a larger-than-life figure in <a href="/wiki/Culture_of_Mongolia" title="Culture of Mongolia">Mongolian culture</a>. He is credited with introducing the <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_script" title="Mongolian script">Mongolian script</a> and creating the first written Mongolian code of law, in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Yassa" title="Yassa">Yassa</a>.
</p><p>During the <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_People%27s_Republic" title="Mongolian People's Republic">communist period</a> in Mongolia, Genghis was often described by the government as a <a href="/wiki/Reactionary" title="Reactionary">reactionary</a> figure, and positive statements about him were avoided.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[90]</a></sup> In 1962, the erection of a monument at his birthplace and a conference held in commemoration of his 800th birthday led to criticism from the Soviet Union and the dismissal of secretary Tömör-Ochir of the ruling <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_People%27s_Party" title="Mongolian People's Party">Mongolian People's Revolutionary Party</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Central_Committee" title="Central Committee">Central Committee</a>.
</p><p>In the early 1990s, the memory of Genghis Khan underwent a powerful revival, partly in reaction to its suppression during the <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_People%27s_Republic" title="Mongolian People's Republic">Mongolian People's Republic</a> period. Genghis Khan became a symbol of national identity for many younger Mongolians, who maintain that the historical records written by non-Mongolians are unfairly biased against Genghis Khan and that his butchery is exaggerated, while his positive role is underrated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[91]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mixed">Mixed</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GenghisKhanMonument.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1636" data-file-width="2848" decoding="async" height="126" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/GenghisKhanMonument.jpg/220px-GenghisKhanMonument.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/GenghisKhanMonument.jpg/330px-GenghisKhanMonument.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/GenghisKhanMonument.jpg/440px-GenghisKhanMonument.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GenghisKhanMonument.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Genghis Khan Monument in <a href="/wiki/Hohhot" title="Hohhot">Hohhot</a>, <a href="/wiki/Inner_Mongolia" title="Inner Mongolia">Inner Mongolia</a>, China</div></div></div>
<p>There are conflicting views of Genghis Khan in China, which suffered a drastic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_in_population" title="Decline in population">decline in population</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-china_95-0"><a href="#cite_note-china-95">[92]</a></sup> The population of north China decreased from 50 million in the 1195 <a href="/wiki/Census" title="Census">census</a> to 8.5 million in the Mongol census of 1235–36; however, many were victims of plague. In <a href="/wiki/Hebei" title="Hebei">Hebei</a> province alone, 9 out of 10 were killed by the <a href="/wiki/Black_Death" title="Black Death">Black Death</a> when <a href="/wiki/Toghon_Tem%C3%BCr" title="Toghon Temür">Toghon Temür</a> was enthroned in 1333.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chua_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chua-96">[93]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="source is a general text not on the Mongol Empire and does not contain any references, also the wiki page on the black death specifically states it is uncertain whether it existed in China at the time (January 2022)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Genghis_Khan#Dubious" title="Talk:Genghis Khan">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template noprint noexcerpt Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:NOTRS" title="Wikipedia:NOTRS"><span title="source is written by Amy Chua, who is not a historian, expert on plague or the black death, the Yuan dynasty, or cites any source for her claims (January 2022)">better source needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Northern China was also struck by floods and famine long after the war in northern China was over in 1234 and not killed by Mongols.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-guide_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-guide-97">[94]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title='source makes no mention of floods, famines, or that "not killed by Mongols" (January 2022)'>failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> The Black Death also contributed. By 1351, two out of three people in China had died of the plague, helping to spur armed rebellion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[95]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability"><span title="source does not mention the black death, nor does it mention two out of three people in China had died of the plague by 1351, a date which is not even mentioned once in the source (January 2022)">failed verification</span></a></i>]</sup> most notably in the form of the <a href="/wiki/Red_Turban_Rebellions" title="Red Turban Rebellions">Red Turban Rebellions</a>. However according to Richard von Glahn, a historian of Chinese economics, China's population only fell by 15% to a third from 1340 to 1370 and there is "a conspicuous lack of evidence for pandemic disease on the scale of the Black Death in China at this time."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEvon_Glahn2016440_99-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEvon_Glahn2016440-99">[96]</a></sup> An unknown number of people also migrated to Southern China in this period,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[97]</a></sup> including under the preceding Southern Song dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaterson2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDtTLDwAAQBAJpgPT88_88]_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaterson2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDtTLDwAAQBAJpgPT88_88]-101">[98]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Mongols also spared many cities from massacre and sacking if they surrendered,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[99]</a></sup> including Kaifeng,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaterson2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDtTLDwAAQBAJpgPT92_92]_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaterson2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDtTLDwAAQBAJpgPT92_92]-103">[100]</a></sup> Yangzhou,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaterson2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDtTLDwAAQBAJpgPT230_230]_104-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaterson2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDtTLDwAAQBAJpgPT230_230]-104">[101]</a></sup> and Hangzhou.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[102]</a></sup> Ethnic Han and Khitan soldiers defected en masse to Genghis Khan against the <a href="/wiki/Jurchen_people" title="Jurchen people">Jurchen</a>-led <a href="/wiki/Jin_dynasty_(1115%E2%80%931234)" title="Jin dynasty (1115–1234)">Jin dynasty</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWaterson2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDtTLDwAAQBAJpgPT84_84]_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWaterson2013[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidDtTLDwAAQBAJpgPT84_84]-106">[103]</a></sup> Equally, while Genghis never conquered all of China, his grandson <a href="/wiki/Kublai_Khan" title="Kublai Khan">Kublai Khan</a>, by completing that conquest and establishing the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>, is often credited with re-uniting China, and there is a great deal of Chinese artwork and literature praising Genghis as a military leader and political genius. The <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a> left an indelible imprint on Chinese political and social structures and a cultural legacy that outshone the preceding Jin dynasty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbbott201454_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbbott201454-107">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Negative">Negative</span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Destruction_under_the_Mongol_Empire" title="Destruction under the Mongol Empire">Destruction under the Mongol Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2786" data-file-width="4429" decoding="async" height="138" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg/220px-DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg/330px-DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg/440px-DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:DiezAlbumsFallOfBaghdad.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Invasions like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Battle_of_Baghdad_(1258)" title="Battle of Baghdad (1258)">the Battle of Baghdad</a> by his grandson are treated as brutal and are seen negatively in Iraq. This illustration is from a 14th-century <a href="/wiki/Jami%27_al-tawarikh" title="Jami' al-tawarikh">Jami' al-tawarikh</a> manuscript.</div></div></div>
<p>The conquests and leadership of Genghis Khan included widespread devastation and <a href="/wiki/Mass_murder" title="Mass murder">mass murder</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJonassohnBjörnson1999276_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJonassohnBjörnson1999276-108">[105]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJacobs1999247–248_109-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJacobs1999247–248-109">[106]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMan2007116–117_110-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMan2007116–117-110">[107]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-111">[108]</a></sup> The targets of campaigns that refused to surrender would often be subject to reprisals in the form of enslavement and wholesale slaughter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-112">[109]</a></sup> The second campaign against <a href="/wiki/Western_Xia" title="Western Xia">Western Xia</a>, the final military action led by Genghis Khan, and during which he died, involved an intentional and systematic destruction of Western Xia cities and culture.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-112">[109]</a></sup> According to <a href="/wiki/John_Man_(author)" title="John Man (author)">John Man</a>, because of this policy of total obliteration, Western Xia is little known to anyone other than experts in the field because so little record is left of that society. He states that "There is a case to be made that this was the first ever recorded example of attempted <a href="/wiki/Genocide" title="Genocide">genocide</a>. It was certainly very successful <i><a href="/wiki/Ethnocide" title="Ethnocide">ethnocide</a></i>."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMan2007116–117_110-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMan2007116–117-110">[107]</a></sup> In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mongol_conquest_of_Khwarezmia" title="Mongol conquest of Khwarezmia">conquest of Khwarezmia</a> under Genghis Khan, the Mongols razed the cities of <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Samarkand" title="Samarkand">Samarkand</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herat" title="Herat">Herāt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tus,_Iran" title="Tus, Iran">Ṭūs</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nishapur" title="Nishapur">Neyshābūr</a> and killed the respective urban populations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[110]</a></sup> His invasions are considered the beginning of a 200-year period known in Iran and other Islamic societies as the "Mongol catastrophe."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_111-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-111">[108]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ali_ibn_al-Athir" title="Ali ibn al-Athir">Ibn al-Athir</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ata-Malik_Juvayni" title="Ata-Malik Juvayni">Ata-Malik Juvaini</a>, Seraj al-Din Jozjani, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashid-al-Din_Hamadani" title="Rashid-al-Din Hamadani">Rashid al-Din Fazl-Allah Hamedani</a>, Iranian historians from the time of Mongol occupation, describe the Mongol invasions as a catastrophe never before seen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_111-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-111">[108]</a></sup> A number of present-day Iranian historians, including <a href="/wiki/Zabihollah_Safa" title="Zabihollah Safa">Zabih Allah Safa</a>, have likewise viewed the period initiated by Genghis Khan as a uniquely catastrophic era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_111-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-111">[108]</a></sup> Steven R. Ward writes that the Mongol violence and depredations in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iranian_Plateau" title="Iranian Plateau">Iranian Plateau</a> "killed up to three-fourths of the population... possibly 10 to 15 million people. Some historians have estimated that Iran's population did not again reach its pre-Mongol levels until the mid-20th century."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[111]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although the famous <a href="/wiki/Mughal_emperors" title="Mughal emperors">Mughal emperors</a> were proud descendants of Genghis Khan and particularly <a href="/wiki/Timur" title="Timur">Timur</a>, they clearly distanced themselves from the Mongol atrocities committed against the Khwarizim Shahs, <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turks</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Persian_people" title="Persian people">Persians</a>, the citizens of <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a> and <a href="/wiki/Damascus" title="Damascus">Damascus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nishapur" title="Nishapur">Nishapur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bukhara" title="Bukhara">Bukhara</a> and historical figures such as <a href="/wiki/Attar_of_Nishapur" title="Attar of Nishapur">Attar of Nishapur</a> and many other notable <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">Muslims</a>.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2020)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> However, Mughal Emperors directly patronized the legacies of Genghis Khan and Timur; together their names were synonymous with the names of other distinguished personalities particularly among the Muslim populations of South Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEAbbott2014507_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAbbott2014507-115">[112]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Depictions">Depictions</span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Emperoryuandinastycollage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1200" data-file-width="1880" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Emperoryuandinastycollage.jpg/280px-Emperoryuandinastycollage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Emperoryuandinastycollage.jpg/420px-Emperoryuandinastycollage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Emperoryuandinastycollage.jpg/560px-Emperoryuandinastycollage.jpg 2x" width="280"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Emperoryuandinastycollage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Genghis Khan and <a href="/wiki/Khagan" title="Khagan">Great Khans</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Yuan_dynasty" title="Yuan dynasty">Yuan dynasty</a>, late 13th and early 14th-century Yuan paintings</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:16th_century_Ottoman_miniature_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="940" data-file-width="1132" decoding="async" height="183" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/16th_century_Ottoman_miniature_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg/220px-16th_century_Ottoman_miniature_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/16th_century_Ottoman_miniature_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg/330px-16th_century_Ottoman_miniature_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/16th_century_Ottoman_miniature_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg/440px-16th_century_Ottoman_miniature_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:16th_century_Ottoman_miniature_of_Genghis_Khan.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>16th century Ottoman miniature of Genghis Khan</div></div></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval">Medieval</span></h3>
<p>Unlike most emperors, Genghis Khan never allowed his image to be portrayed in paintings or sculptures.
</p><p>The earliest known images of Genghis Khan were produced half a century after his death, including the famous <a href="/wiki/National_Palace_Museum" title="National Palace Museum">National Palace Museum</a> portrait in Taiwan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-116"><a href="#cite_note-116">[113]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[114]</a></sup> The portrait portrays Genghis Khan wearing white robes, a leather warming cap and his hair tied in braids, much like a similar depiction of Kublai Khan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[115]</a></sup> This portrait is often considered to represent the closest resemblance to what Genghis Khan actually looked like, though it, like all others renderings, suffers from the same limitation of being, at best, a <a href="/wiki/Facial_composite" title="Facial composite">facial composite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-119">[116]</a></sup> Like many of the earliest images of Genghis Khan, the Chinese-style portrait presents the Great Khan in a manner more akin to a <a href="/wiki/Mandarin_(bureaucrat)" title="Mandarin (bureaucrat)">Mandarin</a> sage than a Mongol warrior.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidA8Y9B5uHQcACpgPA24_24–25],_197_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidA8Y9B5uHQcACpgPA24_24–25],_197-120">[117]</a></sup> Other portrayals of Genghis Khan from other cultures likewise characterized him according to their particular image of him: in Persia he was portrayed as a <a href="/wiki/Turkic_peoples" title="Turkic peoples">Turkic</a> sultan and in Europe he was pictured as an ugly barbarian with a fierce face and cruel eyes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidA8Y9B5uHQcACpgPA25_25]_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidA8Y9B5uHQcACpgPA25_25]-121">[118]</a></sup> According to sinologist <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Giles" title="Herbert Giles">Herbert Allen Giles</a>, a Mongol painter known as Ho-li-hosun (also known as Khorisun or Qooriqosun) was commissioned by Kublai Khan in 1278 to paint the National Palace Museum portrait.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[119]</a></sup> The story goes that Kublai Khan ordered Khorisun, along with the other entrusted remaining followers of Genghis Khan, to ensure the portrait reflected the Great Khan's true image.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[120]</a></sup>
</p><p>The only individuals to have recorded Genghis Khan's physical appearance during his lifetime were the Persian chronicler <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Minhaj-i-Siraj" title="Minhaj-i-Siraj"> Minhaj al-Siraj Juzjani</a> and Chinese diplomat Zhao Hong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[121]</a></sup> Minhaj al-Siraj described Genghis Khan as "a man of tall stature, of vigorous build, robust in body, the hair of his face scanty and turned white, with cats’ eyes, possessed of dedicated energy, discernment, genius, and understanding, awe-striking...".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidA8Y9B5uHQcACpgPA6_6]_125-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeatherford2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidA8Y9B5uHQcACpgPA6_6]-125">[122]</a></sup> The chronicler had also previously commented on Genghis Khan's height, powerful build, with cat's eyes and lack of grey hair, based on the evidence of eyes witnesses in 1220, which saw Genghis Khan fighting in the Khorasan (modern day northwest Persia).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mote_2003_433_126-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mote_2003_433-126">[123]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[124]</a></sup> According to Paul Ratchnevsky, the <a href="/wiki/Song_dynasty" title="Song dynasty">Song dynasty</a> envoy Zhao Hong who visited the Mongols in 1221,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[125]</a></sup> described Genghis Khan as "of tall and majestic stature, his brow is broad and his beard is long".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mote_2003_433_126-1"><a href="#cite_note-Mote_2003_433-126">[123]</a></sup>
</p><p>Other descriptions of Genghis Khan come from 14th century texts. The Persian historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rashid-al-Din_Hamadani" title="Rashid-al-Din Hamadani">Rashid-al-Din</a> in <i><a href="/wiki/Jami%27_al-tawarikh" title="Jami' al-tawarikh">Jami' al-tawarikh</a></i>, written in the beginning of the 14th century, stated that most Borjigin ancestors of Genghis Khan were "tall, long-bearded, red-haired, and bluish green-eyed," features which Genghis Khan himself had. The factual nature of this statement is considered controversial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_119-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-119">[116]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/The_Georgian_Chronicles" title="The Georgian Chronicles">Georgian Chronicles</a>, in a passage written in the 14th century, Genghis Khan is similarly described as a large, good-looking man, with red hair.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[126]</a></sup> However, according to <a href="/wiki/John_Andrew_Boyle" title="John Andrew Boyle">John Andrew Boyle</a>, Rashid al-Din's text of red hair referred to ruddy skin complexion, and that Genghis Khan was of ruddy complexion like most of his children except for Kublai Khan who was swarthy. He translated the text as “It chanced that he was born 2 months before Möge, and when Chingiz-Khan's eye fell upon him he said: “all our children are of a ruddy complexion, but this child is swarthy like his maternal uncles. Tell Sorqoqtani Beki to give him to a good nurse to be reared”.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[127]</a></sup> 14th century Arabic historian <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Shihab_al-Umari" title="Shihab al-Umari">Shihab al-Umari</a> also disputed Rashid al-Din's translation and claimed <a href="/wiki/Alan_Gua" title="Alan Gua">Alan Gua</a> falsified the origin of her clan.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_131-0"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-131">[128]</a></sup> Some Historians such as Denise Aigle claimed that Rashid al-Din mythicized the origin of Genghis Khan ancestors (the Borjigin clan) through his own interpretations of <a href="/wiki/The_Secret_History_of_the_Mongols" title="The Secret History of the Mongols">The Secret History of the Mongols</a>. Italian historian <a href="/wiki/Igor_de_Rachewiltz" title="Igor de Rachewiltz">Igor de Rachewiltz</a> claimed that the Mongol origins of the early ancestors of Genghis Khan were animals born from the blue eye wolf (Borte Chino) and the fallow doe (Qo'ai Maral) that was described in the early legends, that their ancestors were animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-books.google.com_131-1"><a href="#cite_note-books.google.com-131">[128]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Modern">Modern</span></h3>
<p>In Mongolia today, Genghis Khan's name and likeness appear on products, streets, buildings, and other places. His face can be found on everyday commodities, from liquor bottles to candy, and on the largest denominations of 500, 1,000, 5,000, 10,000, and 20,000 <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_t%C3%B6gr%C3%B6g" title="Mongolian tögrög">Mongolian tögrög</a> (₮).
</p><p>Mongolia's main international airport in <a href="/wiki/Ulaanbaatar" title="Ulaanbaatar">Ulaanbaatar</a> is named <a href="/wiki/Buyant-Ukhaa_International_Airport" title="Buyant-Ukhaa International Airport">Chinggis Khaan International Airport</a>, and there is a 40m-high <a href="/wiki/Equestrian_statue_of_Genghis_Khan" title="Equestrian statue of Genghis Khan">equestrian statue of Genghis Khan</a> east of the Mongolian capital. There has been talk about regulating the use of his name and image to avoid trivialization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-132"><a href="#cite_note-132">[129]</a></sup>
</p><p>Genghis Khan's birthday, on the first day of winter (according to the <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_calendar" title="Mongolian calendar">Mongolian lunar calendar</a>), is a national holiday.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[130]</a></sup>
</p><p>Outside of Mongolia, there have been numerous works of literature, films and other adaptation works based on the Mongolian ruler and his legacy.
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span></h4>
<ul><li>"<a href="/wiki/The_Squire%27s_Tale" title="The Squire's Tale">The Squire's Tale</a>", one of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Canterbury_Tales" title="The Canterbury Tales">The Canterbury Tales</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Geoffrey_Chaucer" title="Geoffrey Chaucer">Geoffrey Chaucer</a>, is set at the court of Genghis Khan.</li>
<li><i>Genghis Khan</i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[131]</a></sup> by <a href="/wiki/Vasily_Yan" title="Vasily Yan">Vasily Yan</a>, 1939—first installment of an epic trilogy about the conquests of the Mongols.</li>
<li><i>The End of Genghis</i>, a poem by <a href="/wiki/F._L._Lucas" title="F. L. Lucas">F. L. Lucas</a>, in which the dying Khan, attended by his <a href="/wiki/Khitan_people" title="Khitan people">Khitan</a> counsellor <a href="/wiki/Yel%C3%BC_Chucai" title="Yelü Chucai">Yelü Chucai</a>, looks back on his life.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-135"><a href="#cite_note-135">[132]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Conqueror_(novel_series)" title="Conqueror (novel series)">The Conqueror</a></i> series of novels by <a href="/wiki/Conn_Iggulden" title="Conn Iggulden">Conn Iggulden</a></li>
<li><i>Steppe</i> by <a href="/wiki/Piers_Anthony" title="Piers Anthony">Piers Anthony</a></li>
<li><i>Genghis Khan</i> (Last incarnation) in <a href="/wiki/Metro_2033_(novel)" title="Metro 2033 (novel)">Metro 2033</a> by <a href="/wiki/Dmitry_Glukhovsky" title="Dmitry Glukhovsky">Dmitry Glukhovsky</a></li>
<li><i>White cloud of Genghis Khan</i> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chingiz_Aitmatov" title="Chingiz Aitmatov">Chingiz Aitmatov</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[133]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Private_Life_of_Genghis_Khan" title="The Private Life of Genghis Khan">The Private Life of Genghis Khan</a></i> by <a href="/wiki/Douglas_Adams" title="Douglas Adams">Douglas Adams</a> and <a href="/wiki/Graham_Chapman" title="Graham Chapman">Graham Chapman</a></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Films">Films</span></h4>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan_(1950_film)" title="Genghis Khan (1950 film)">Genghis Khan</a></i>, a 1950 Philippine film directed by <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Conde" title="Manuel Conde">Manuel Conde</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/The_Conqueror_(1956_film)" title="The Conqueror (1956 film)">The Conqueror</a></i>, released in 1956 and starring <a href="/wiki/John_Wayne" title="John Wayne">John Wayne</a> as Temüjin and <a href="/wiki/Susan_Hayward" title="Susan Hayward">Susan Hayward</a> as Börte.</li>
<li><i>Changez Khan</i>, a 1957 Indian Hindi-language film directed by Kedar Kapoor, starring <a href="/wiki/Sheikh_Mukhtar" title="Sheikh Mukhtar">Sheikh Mukhtar</a> as the emperor along with <a href="/wiki/Bina_Rai" title="Bina Rai">Bina Rai</a> and <a href="/wiki/Prem_Nath" title="Prem Nath">Prem Nath</a> in the lead roles.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[134]</a></sup></li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan_(1965_film)" title="Genghis Khan (1965 film)">Genghis Khan</a></i>, a 1965 film starring <a href="/wiki/Omar_Sharif" title="Omar Sharif">Omar Sharif</a>.</li>
<li><i>Under the Eternal Blue Sky</i>, a Mongolian film directed by Baljinnyam, which was released in 1990. Starring <a href="/wiki/Agvaantserengiin_Enkhtaivan" title="Agvaantserengiin Enkhtaivan">Agvaantserengiin Enkhtaivan</a> as Temüjin.</li>
<li><i>Genghis Khan</i>, an unfinished 1992 film starring <a href="/wiki/Richard_Tyson" title="Richard Tyson">Richard Tyson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charlton_Heston" title="Charlton Heston">Charlton Heston</a> and <a href="/wiki/Pat_Morita" title="Pat Morita">Pat Morita</a>.</li>
<li><i>Genghis Khan – A Proud Son Of Heaven</i>, a 1998 film made in Mongolian, with English subtitles.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan:_To_the_Ends_of_the_Earth_and_Sea" title="Genghis Khan: To the Ends of the Earth and Sea">Genghis Khan: To the Ends of the Earth and Sea</a></i>, also known as <i>The Descendant of Gray Wolf</i>, a Japanese-Mongolian film released in 2007.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Mongol_(film)" title="Mongol (film)">Mongol</a></i>, a 2007 film directed by <a href="/wiki/Sergei_Bodrov" title="Sergei Bodrov">Sergei Bodrov</a>, starring <a href="/wiki/Tadanobu_Asano" title="Tadanobu Asano">Tadanobu Asano</a>. (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> nominee for Best Foreign Language Film).</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/No_Right_to_Die_%E2%80%93_Chinggis_Khaan" title="No Right to Die – Chinggis Khaan">No Right to Die – Chinggis Khaan</a></i>, a Mongolian film released in 2008.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan_(2018_film)" title="Genghis Khan (2018 film)">Genghis Khan</a></i>, a Chinese film released in 2018.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Television_series">Television series</span></h4>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan_(TVB_TV_series)" title="Genghis Khan (TVB TV series)">Genghis Khan</a></i>, a 1987 Hong Kong television series produced by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Television_Broadcasts_Limited" title="Television Broadcasts Limited">TVB</a>, starring <a href="/wiki/Alex_Man" title="Alex Man">Alex Man</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan_(ATV_TV_series)" title="Genghis Khan (ATV TV series)">Genghis Khan</a></i>, a 1987 Hong Kong television series produced by <a href="/wiki/Asia_Television" title="Asia Television">ATV</a>, starring <a href="/wiki/Tony_Liu" title="Tony Liu">Tony Liu</a>.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan_(2004_TV_series)" title="Genghis Khan (2004 TV series)">Genghis Khan</a></i>, a 2004 Chinese-Mongolian co-produced television series, starring <a href="/wiki/Batdorj-in_Baasanjab" title="Batdorj-in Baasanjab">Ba Sen</a>, who is a descendant of Genghis Khan's second son <a href="/wiki/Chagatai_Khan" title="Chagatai Khan">Chagatai</a>.</li>
<li>"Aaakhri Chattan", a 1978 Pakistani drama series having Zahoor Ahmed as Genghis Khan.</li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span></h4>
<ul><li>West German pop band <a href="/wiki/Dschinghis_Khan" title="Dschinghis Khan">Dschinghis Khan</a> took its name from the German-language spelling of Genghis Khan. They participated in the <a href="/wiki/Eurovision_Song_Contest_1979" title="Eurovision Song Contest 1979">Eurovision Song Contest 1979</a> with <a href="/wiki/Dschinghis_Khan_(song)" title="Dschinghis Khan (song)">their song of the same name</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_music" title="Heavy metal music">Heavy metal</a> band <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iron_Maiden_(band)" title="Iron Maiden (band)">Iron Maiden</a> released an all-instrumental track titled "Genghis Khan" on their 1981 sophomore album <i><a href="/wiki/Killers_(Iron_Maiden_album)" title="Killers (Iron Maiden album)">Killers</a></i>.</li>
<li>The band <a href="/wiki/Miike_Snow" title="Miike Snow">Miike Snow</a> released the song "<a href="/wiki/Genghis_Khan_(Miike_Snow_song)" title="Genghis Khan (Miike Snow song)">Genghis Khan</a>" in 2017.</li>
<li>Mongolian Folk-Rock band <a href="/wiki/The_Hu" title="The Hu">The Hu</a> released a song called <i>The Great Chinggis Khaan</i> in August 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[135]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Video_games">Video games</span></h4>
<ul><li><i><a href="/wiki/Tem%C3%BCjin_(video_game)" title="Temüjin (video game)">Temüjin (video game)</a></i>, a 1997 computer game.</li>
<li><i><a href="/wiki/Aoki_%C5%8Ckami_to_Shiroki_Mejika" title="Aoki Ōkami to Shiroki Mejika">Aoki Ōkami to Shiroki Mejika</a></i>, Genghis Khan-themed Japanese game series.</li></ul>

font-weight:normal; font-size: 1em; line-height: 1.2em; vertical-align:middle; middle">ᠲᠡᠮᠦᠵᠢᠨ</span>; <a href="/wiki/Mongolian_language" title="Mongolian language">Mongolian</a>: <span lang="mn">Тэмүжин</span>, <small>romanized: </small><span title="Mongolian-language romanization"><i lang="mn-Latn">Temüjin</i></span> <small>Mongolian pronunciation: </small><span class="IPA" lang="mn-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Mongolian" title="Help:IPA/Mongolian">[tʰemut͡ʃiŋ]</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Simplified_Chinese_characters" title="Simplified Chinese characters">simplified Chinese</a>: <span lang="zh-Hans"><a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%93%81%E6%9C%A8%E7%9C%9F" title="wikt:铁木真">铁木真</a></span>; <a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-pinyin">Tiěmùzhēn</span></i>; <a href="/wiki/Wade%E2%80%93Giles" title="Wade–Giles">Wade–Giles</a>: <i><span lang="zh-Latn-wadegile">T'ieh<sup>3</sup>-mu<sup>4</sup>-chen<sup>1</sup></span></i>.</span>
